### DAY 17 — The Anatomy of Negotiated Peace: Treaties & Compromise
**Grammar Focus:** Cleft + Inverted Conditional Combo

#### 📚 Detail Materi

**Target Kosakata (Box of Words):**

| No | Kosakata + Phonetic UK | English Definition | Catatan Indonesia |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1 | ratification /ˌrætɪfɪˈkeɪʃən/ | formal legislative or governmental approval of a treaty — distinct from signing, which comes before | – |
| 2 | sovereignty /ˈsɒvrənti/ | the supreme authority of a state over its own territory and political affairs | – |
| 3 | ceasefire /ˈsiːsfaɪər/ | a temporary halt to armed conflict, typically used as a stepping stone towards formal peace negotiations | – |
| 4 | reparations /ˌrepəˈreɪʃənz/ | payments or restitution required of a guilty party to compensate for damage, injustice, or losses caused | – |
| 5 | self-determination /ˈself dɪˌtɜːmɪˈneɪʃən/ | the right of a people or nation to determine their own political status and form of governance | – |
| 6 | asymmetric /eɪˈsɪmetrɪk/ | characterised by a significant imbalance in power, resources, or leverage — as between negotiating parties | – |
| 7 | annexation /ˌænekˈseɪʃən/ | the forcible or unilateral incorporation of territory into a political entity — typically contested internationally | – |
| 8 | it was [X] that /ɪt wɒz [X] ðæt/ | it-cleft: puts emphatic focus on one key element — "It was the failure to address sovereignty that derailed ratification." | – |
| 9 | what [prevented/enabled] was /wɒt/ | what-cleft: fronts the key cause or outcome — "What prevented a lasting ceasefire was the unresolved question of annexation." | – |
| 10 | all that remained /ɔːl ðæt rɪˈmeɪnd/ | all-cleft: signals what was left after everything else failed — "All that remained was a framework too fragile to survive domestic pressure." | – |
| 11 | had it not been for /hæd ɪt nɒt biːn fɔː/ | negative past inverted conditional: "Had it not been for the guarantor's intervention, ratification would have collapsed entirely." | – |
| 12 | were it not for /wɜːr ɪt nɒt fɔː/ | negative present/hypothetical inverted conditional: "Were it not for the sovereignty dispute, the reparations framework would be considerably less contested." | – |
| 13 | it is no exaggeration to say /ɪt ɪz nəʊ ɪɡˌzædʒəˈreɪʃən tə seɪ/ | emphatic framing: "It is no exaggeration to say that what ultimately preserved the ceasefire was the asymmetric pressure applied by guarantor states." | – |
| 14 | preamble /ˈpriːæmbl/ | the introductory section of a treaty, setting out its purpose, principles, and aspirations — binding in spirit if not always in law | – |
| 15 | guarantor /ˌɡærənˈtɔːr/ | a state or entity that formally vouches for the terms of an agreement and assumes responsibility for ensuring its implementation | – |

**Grammar Pendukung (C1):**
- **Cleft Sentences** — a grammatical structure that splits a simple sentence into two clauses to place emphatic focus on one specific element. Three main types at C1:

  | Cleft Type | Structure | Example |
  |---|---|---|
  | **it-cleft** | It + be + [focus] + that/who + rest | "It was the asymmetric power balance that made genuine self-determination impossible." |
  | **what-cleft** | What + [clause] + be + [focus] | "What the preamble failed to address was the status of annexed territories." |
  | **all-cleft** | All + [clause] + be + [focus] | "All that the guarantors could agree on was a temporary ceasefire." |

  Clefts are not just stylistic — they signal which element of the argument the writer considers most analytically significant. In peace treaty analysis, they direct the reader's attention to what actually mattered, rather than stating everything with equal weight.

- **Inverted Conditional — Negative Forms (C1 new)**: Day 12 taught "had/were/should" inversion. Day 17 extends this to the **negative** form — the most formal, highest-register inverted conditional:

  | Form | "If" Equivalent | Example |
  |---|---|---|
  | **Had it not been for** + noun | If it had not been for | "Had it not been for the guarantor's role, the ratification process would have failed." |
  | **Were it not for** + noun | If it were not for | "Were it not for the sovereignty dispute, reparations would be the central sticking point." |

  These negative inversions are more formal and analytically specific than "Had they only..." (D12) — they identify a single condition whose absence would have produced a different outcome.

- **Cleft + Inverted Conditional COMBO** — the highest-register analytical technique for historical and political analysis:
  - "**What** derailed the peace process **was** the failure to agree on self-determination — **had it not been for** the annexation dispute, the preamble would have been signed within months."
  - "**It was** asymmetric power **that** made genuine sovereignty negotiations impossible; **were it not for** the guarantors' external pressure, the ceasefire would never have held."
  - "**All that remained** was a hollow framework — **it is no exaggeration to say** that **had it not been for** the reparations clause, even this would have been rejected."

- **B2 → C1 contrast:**
  - B2: "The main reason the peace talks failed was because of the sovereignty issue."
  - C1 Cleft: "What ultimately undermined the talks was the unresolved question of sovereignty."
  - C1 Cleft + Inverted Cond.: "What ultimately undermined the talks was the sovereignty question — had it not been for the guarantors' insistence on a ceasefire framework, the process would have collapsed entirely."

**Konteks Penggunaan:**
Treaties, peace negotiations, and diplomatic history are analysed through causality — what happened, what caused it, what would have changed it. Cleft sentences identify the single most important causal element; inverted conditionals explore the counterfactual. Together, they are the analytical grammar of post-conflict analysis, diplomatic history, and international relations commentary.

---

#### ✅ Detail Task

**📖 Mode Cerita:**
A retrospective analysis of a specific peace negotiation — real or historical — examining what succeeded, what failed, and what would have changed the outcome. The analysis uses cleft structures to identify key causes and inverted conditionals to explore the counterfactual.
Example: "**It was** the failure to address the status of annexed territories in the preamble **that** transformed a tentative ceasefire into a permanent stalemate. **What** the guarantor states ultimately provided **was** not enforcement power but a face-saving framework within which asymmetric concessions could be made to appear mutual. **All that remained**, when the ratification process finally collapsed, was a sovereignty claim neither side could publicly relinquish — **had it not been for** the reparations clause, even this minimal framework would have been rejected immediately."
Write 8–10 sentences analysing a specific peace process, treaty, or negotiation — real or hypothetical. Use at least 5 words from the Box of Words, at least 2 cleft constructions (it was X that / what X was / all that remained), and at least 1 negative inverted conditional (had it not been for / were it not for). Grammar utama: Cleft + Inverted Conditional Combo.
1. ...
2. ...
3. ...
4. ...
5. ...
6. ...
7. ...
8. ...
(add sentence 9 and 10 if you want)

**⚡ Mode Kilat:**
1 analytical sentence per word — use at least 2 cleft constructions and 1 inverted conditional across your 5 sentences:
- ratification: ...
- sovereignty: ...
- reparations: ...
- asymmetric: ...
- guarantor: ...

**Speaking Challenge (3 Pertanyaan):**
1. **It was the question of self-determination that made the peace process so fragile. Had it not been for international guarantors, what outcome would have been most likely — and is it no exaggeration to say that sovereignty disputes remain the single most difficult issue in any peace negotiation?**
2. **Were it not for asymmetric power between parties, would peace negotiations be genuinely more equitable — or would they simply surface different kinds of sticking point? What does the historical record suggest?**
3. **What, in your view, makes a peace treaty last — not the preamble, not the reparations clause, but the underlying condition without which no framework can hold?**

---

#### 🔍 Detail Review & Koreksi (8 Langkah)

**Langkah 1 — Koreksi Writing:**
Common cleft and negative inversion errors at C1:
1. **it-cleft tense agreement**: "It was the guarantors' intervention **that prevents** collapse" ❌ → the that-clause must match the time reference: "It was the guarantors' intervention **that prevented** collapse" ✅ — if the main verb is past, the that-clause should also be past.
2. **what-cleft without matching auxiliary**: "What the parties needed was was achieved" ❌ — the what-cleft should not repeat the verb twice. Restructure: "What the parties needed was never achieved" ✅.
3. **Had it not been for + verb instead of noun**: "Had it not been for **they intervened**" ❌ → "Had it not been for **their intervention**" ✅ — negative inversion requires a noun phrase after "for", not a clause.
4. **Were it not for** misused in past contexts: "Were it not for the annexation **that occurred in 1945**..." — this implies the annexation is still ongoing (present hypothetical). For past counterfactuals, use "Had it not been for..."

**Langkah 2 — Analisa BOW:**
- **ratification vs signing**: treaties are typically **signed** (symbolic political commitment) and then **ratified** (formal legal adoption by legislature). A treaty can be signed but not ratified — which means it has no legal force. This distinction matters in analysis: "the preamble was signed but ratification was blocked in parliament."
- **ceasefire vs armistice vs peace treaty**: "ceasefire" = temporary, informal stop; "armistice" = formal suspension of hostilities (end of fighting, not resolution); "peace treaty" = comprehensive formal settlement. In historical analysis, misusing these signals a lack of precision.
- **guarantor**: note the British spelling ("-or", not "-er"). Plural: guarantors. Collocations: "external guarantor", "guarantor state", "serve as guarantor", "guarantor of the agreement."
- **asymmetric**: most natural in compounds — "asymmetric power", "asymmetric warfare", "asymmetric negotiation". Less natural as a standalone adjective ("the negotiation was asymmetric") — prefer "the negotiation was characterised by asymmetric power."
- Vocab refresh from Day 16 (1-2 words): "it is vital that / cannot be ruled out" — e.g., "It is vital that the preamble **be** drafted with sufficient ambiguity; without it, ratification cannot be ruled out as the first casualty of domestic opposition."

**Langkah 3 — Analisa CEFR:**
Template: "The what-cleft is working precisely — 'what prevented a lasting settlement was' is directing me to the specific cause you consider most analytically significant, not just cataloguing factors. The negative inversion 'had it not been for' is formal and correct. For C2: (1) embed a cleft inside a negative inversion — 'Had it not been for what was ultimately the guarantors' most significant concession, the ratification process would have collapsed'; (2) use a what-cleft to open a paragraph and an inverted conditional to close it — the cleft identifies the cause, the conditional explores the counterfactual."

**Langkah 4 — 5W+1H:**
| Elemen | Versi User | Versi Lebih Lengkap |
|---|---|---|
| What | A peace negotiation failed or partially succeeded | A ratification process in which what prevented success was the asymmetric power dynamic — not the absence of a ceasefire, but the absence of sovereignty guarantees |
| When | during the negotiations | at the critical moment when the preamble had been agreed but reparations remained unresolved |
| Where | (not mentioned) | between parties for whom the annexation dispute was not negotiable — guarantors notwithstanding |
| Why | the parties disagreed | it was the failure to separate self-determination from reparations that made any framework politically unsustainable |
| Who | the negotiating parties | the stronger party, whose asymmetric leverage made what-cleft analysis essential: what they offered was not compromise but the appearance of it |
| How | through failed compromise | had it not been for the guarantors' insistence on a ceasefire framework, all that would have remained was sovereignty rhetoric on both sides |

**Enriched Paragraph:**
"**It was** the unresolved annexation dispute **that** made ratification politically impossible for the weaker party — a government unable to sign away sovereign territory without domestic collapse. **What** the preamble had conspicuously failed to address **was** the question of self-determination, and **all that remained** after months of negotiation was a ceasefire framework dependent on external guarantors with no genuine enforcement capacity. **Were it not for** the asymmetric leverage the stronger state retained throughout, it is no exaggeration to say that the reparations clause alone would have been sufficient grounds for agreement. **Had it not been for** the guarantors' insistence on preserving even this fragile architecture, the talks would have ended without a preamble, without reparations, and without a framework of any kind."

**Langkah 5 — Discourse Markers:**
Cleft + Inverted Conditional for Peace Negotiations:
- **It was [X] that** → "It was the asymmetric power dynamic that ultimately determined the shape of the ceasefire, not the parties' preferences."
- **What [X] was** → "What the guarantors provided was not a solution but a framework within which a sovereign compromise could be named."
- **Had it not been for** → "Had it not been for the reparations framework, the ratification process would have had nothing to ratify."

**Langkah 6 — Craft & Lexical Chunks:**
*(Lihat PROMPT block — Kak Ara menyiapkan lexical chunks dan craft feedback spesifik topik hari ini.)*

**Langkah 7 — Register/Formality Check:**
Flag: it-cleft tense mismatch in that-clause; "had it not been for" followed by a clause instead of a noun phrase; "were it not for" used for past counterfactual (should be "had it not been for"); "ceasefire" used interchangeably with "peace treaty" (different stages of resolution). ✅ skip if consistent.

**Langkah 8 — Speaking Challenge:**
Q1 uses an it-cleft in the question itself ("It was the question of self-determination that...") + "had it not been for" + "is it no exaggeration to say". Q2 uses "were it not for" to invite counter-cleft reasoning. Q3 is an open what-cleft question that invites the user to construct their own cleft answer.

---

#### 💬 Detail Feedback & Panduan Kak Ara

**Tone & Bahasa:**
Historically grounded and analytically precise. "'What prevented a lasting settlement was' is pointing me to the specific cause you find most significant. That focus is what clefts do — they're not just style, they're argument architecture." "Watch the what-cleft: the 'what' clause needs to be a complete verb phrase, not just a noun."

**Error Umum Topik Ini:**
1. it-cleft tense mismatch — that-clause must agree with the main verb's time
2. "Had it not been for" followed by a clause (needs a noun phrase)
3. "Were it not for" used for completed past events (needs "had it not been for")
4. "Ceasefire" used synonymously with "peace treaty" — different stages
5. "Ratification" confused with "signing" — ratification is the legal step, not the symbolic one

**Template Pujian (Akhir Sesi):**
"Excellent work, [PANGGILAN] [NAMA]. The cleft structures are doing analytical work, not just adding elegance — 'what prevented X was Y' is telling me *exactly* what you consider the decisive factor. The negative inversion 'had it not been for' pairs with the cleft beautifully. This is the kind of writing that reads like serious historical analysis."

**Saran Naik Level (C1 → C2):**
"For C2, embed a cleft inside a negative inverted conditional — 'Had it not been for what was ultimately the guarantors' single most consequential intervention, the ceasefire framework would never have held.' That nesting (inverted conditional + what-cleft inside it) is a C2 signature in formal analytical writing. The reader must track two emphatic structures simultaneously — and reward the writer for making both work."

---

#### 🗣️ Detail Practice (Speaking Mode)

**Skenario Speaking:**
The user is speaking as a historian, policy analyst, or international relations specialist — commenting on a specific peace process, treaty, or failed negotiation with precision and historical authority.

**Pertanyaan Lanjutan (jika user lancar):**
1. Had it not been for the asymmetric power dynamic you've described, would self-determination have been easier to achieve — or would it simply have been a different party exercising dominance?
2. What-cleft challenge: frame your most important analytical point about the negotiation you described using "What X ultimately was, was Y" — then defend it.

**Pattern Drill (jika user macet):**
- It-cleft: It was [X] that [consequence/cause].
  → It was the sovereignty dispute that made ratification impossible for the domestic legislature.
- What-cleft: What [subject + verb] was [focus].
  → What the guarantor states ultimately ensured was not peace but the appearance of it.
- Had it not been for: Had it not been for [noun phrase], [past conditional result].
  → Had it not been for the ceasefire framework, the reparations talks would never have begun.

### DAY 18 — Whistleblowing: Loyalty vs the Greater Good
**Grammar Focus:** Participle + Ellipsis Combo

#### 📚 Detail Materi

**Target Kosakata (Box of Words):**

| No | Kosakata + Phonetic UK | English Definition | Catatan Indonesia |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1 | whistleblower /ˈwɪslˌbləʊər/ | a person who exposes wrongdoing within an organisation, typically at significant personal or professional risk | – |
| 2 | retaliation /rɪˌtæliˈeɪʃən/ | action taken against someone as punishment or revenge for disclosing wrongdoing — often institutional in character | – |
| 3 | non-disclosure agreement /nɒn dɪsˈkləʊʒər əˈɡriːmənt/ | a legal contract preventing an individual from revealing information about an organisation — commonly abbreviated NDA | – |
| 4 | public interest /ˈpʌblɪk ˈɪntrəst/ | the general wellbeing of the public — used to justify disclosure against institutional confidentiality obligations | – |
| 5 | complicity /kəmˈplɪsɪti/ | involvement in wrongdoing, including through deliberate silence or inaction — awareness that does not act | – |
| 6 | institutional loyalty /ˌɪnstɪˈtjuːʃənl ˈlɔɪəlti/ | allegiance to an organisation or employer, sometimes maintained at the cost of moral or legal obligations | – |
| 7 | protected disclosure /prəˈtektɪd dɪsˈkləʊʒər/ | a legally recognised category of whistleblowing that provides specific protections against retaliation for the discloser | – |
| 8 | having exposed /ˈhævɪŋ ɪkˈspəʊzd/ | perfect participle: action completed before the main clause — "Having exposed the cover-up, she faced immediate retaliation." | – |
| 9 | seen as /siːn æz/ | reduced passive participle / ellipsis: "Seen as a threat, most whistleblowers face institutional pressure to remain silent." | – |
| 10 | when faced with /wen feɪst wɪð/ | adverbial participle (time/condition): "When faced with institutional loyalty, employees rarely choose disclosure." | – |
| 11 | forced to choose between /fɔːst tə tʃuːz bɪˈtwiːn/ | passive participle ellipsis: "Forced to choose between loyalty and public interest, she opted for a protected disclosure." | – |
| 12 | rather than [verb-ing] /ˈrɑːðər ðæn/ | ellipsis of second subject + auxiliary: "Rather than violating the NDA, many employees absorb complicity silently." | – |
| 13 | while acknowledging /waɪl əkˈnɒlɪdʒɪŋ/ | adverbial participle (concession): "While acknowledging the risks, she proceeded with the protected disclosure." | – |
| 14 | in breach of /ɪn briːtʃ ɒv/ | formal: acting in violation of — "The organisation acted in breach of its publicly stated commitment to transparency." | – |
| 15 | moral courage /ˈmɒrəl ˈkʌrɪdʒ/ | the willingness to act rightly in the face of personal risk, social pressure, or sustained institutional opposition | – |

**Grammar Pendukung (C1):**
- **Participle Clauses** — non-finite clauses that allow two pieces of information to be combined economically, without repeating a subject or auxiliary:

  | Participle Type | When Used | Example |
  |---|---|---|
  | **Present participle** (-ing) | simultaneous action | "Facing certain retaliation, the whistleblower weighed the cost of silence." |
  | **Past participle** (passive meaning) | result/state | "Seen as a threat, she found institutional doors closing around her." |
  | **Perfect participle** (having + past participle) | prior, completed action | "Having exposed the wrongdoing, she was left with no institutional protection." |
  | **Adverbial participle** (when/while/once + -ing/past participle) | time, condition, concession | "When faced with an NDA, most employees defer to institutional loyalty rather than public interest." |

- **Ellipsis** — the deliberate omission of words that can be inferred from context, creating more concise, sophisticated prose:

  | Ellipsis Type | Full Form | Ellipsis Form |
  |---|---|---|
  | Reduced relative clause | "the employee who was seen as a threat" | "the employee **seen as a threat**" |
  | Subject ellipsis (coordinated) | "She disclosed the data and **she** faced retaliation" | "She disclosed the data and [she] faced retaliation" |
  | Rather than + gerund | "Rather than **choosing to** violate the NDA" | "Rather than violating the NDA" |
  | While + participle | "While **she was** acknowledging the risks" | "While acknowledging the risks" |
  | Forced to (passive ellipt.) | "**Having been** forced to choose between..." | "Forced to choose between..." |

- **Participle + Ellipsis COMBO** — combining a participle clause with ellipsis in the same sentence creates maximum analytical density without wordiness — the hallmark of quality C1+ academic writing:
  - "**Having exposed** the wrongdoing [perfect participle] and **subsequently denied** [ellipsis of "having been"] any institutional protection, the whistleblower's case became the defining test of protected disclosure law."
  - "**Seen as a threat** [reduced passive participle] **rather than** [ellipsis] as a public interest actor, most whistleblowers find institutional loyalty weaponised against them."
  - "**When faced with** retaliation [adverbial participle, 'she' ellipted] and **forced to choose** [coordinated participle, ellipsis of "when forced"] between complicity and moral courage, the cost of disclosure becomes impossible to calculate."

- **B2 → C1 contrast:**
  - B2: "Because she had exposed the wrongdoing, she was in a vulnerable position. She had to choose between loyalty and public interest."
  - C1 Combo: "**Having exposed** the wrongdoing and **forced to choose** between institutional loyalty and public interest, she found herself in a position of vulnerability that protected disclosure law had never been designed to address."

**Konteks Penggunaan:**
Whistleblowing exists at the intersection of law, ethics, loyalty, and power. The grammar of this Day — participles and ellipsis — is not coincidental to the topic: analysing whistleblowing requires the same economy and precision that makes participle + ellipsis valuable. Both compress: the whistleblower compresses a complex moral decision into one act; the grammar compresses a complex causal chain into one sentence. This is the grammar of moral analysis under constraint.

---

#### ✅ Detail Task

**📖 Mode Cerita:**
A moral and analytical account of a whistleblowing case — real or fictional — examining the tension between institutional loyalty and the public interest, and the cost of moral courage. The account should use participle clauses to show causal sequence and ellipsis to achieve analytical density.
Example: "**Having signed** an NDA and **accepted** institutional loyalty as the condition of employment, the whistleblower faced a decision that no regulatory framework had fully anticipated. **Seen as a threat** rather than as a public interest actor, he found himself denied the protection that protected disclosure law was supposed to provide — **forced to choose** between complicity and retaliation, with neither option offering anything resembling justice."
Write 8–10 sentences analysing a whistleblowing case — real or fictional — examining what moral courage requires and what institutional loyalty demands. Use at least 5 words from the Box of Words, at least 2 participle constructions (having exposed / seen as / when faced with / forced to choose between / while acknowledging), and at least 2 ellipsis patterns (rather than / reduced relative clause / coordinated clause ellipsis). Grammar utama: Participle + Ellipsis Combo.
1. ...
2. ...
3. ...
4. ...
5. ...
6. ...
7. ...
8. ...
(add sentence 9 and 10 if you want)

**⚡ Mode Kilat:**
1 formal sentence per word — use at least 2 participle constructions and 1 ellipsis pattern across your 5 sentences:
- whistleblower: ...
- complicity: ...
- in breach of: ...
- moral courage: ...
- protected disclosure: ...

**Speaking Challenge (3 Pertanyaan):**
1. **Having considered the legal and institutional risks, would you personally make a protected disclosure — or, faced with retaliation and the loss of institutional loyalty, would the weight of complicity feel preferable?**
2. **Seen as a threat by their institutions, most whistleblowers face retaliation rather than protection. In light of this, does a non-disclosure agreement function as a legitimate legal instrument — or as a mechanism of complicity?**
3. **What distinguishes moral courage from recklessness? When faced with a choice between institutional loyalty and the greater good, how does one know the greater good is actually greater?**

---

#### 🔍 Detail Review & Koreksi (8 Langkah)

**Langkah 1 — Koreksi Writing:**
Common participle and ellipsis errors at C1:
1. **Dangling participle**: "Having exposed the wrongdoing, the institution retaliated" ❌ — the subject of "having exposed" must be the same as the main clause subject: "Having exposed the wrongdoing, **she** faced immediate retaliation" ✅. If the subjects differ, the participle is dangling (and the sentence is ambiguous or wrong).
2. **Perfect participle vs present participle timing**: "Facing retaliation for exposing the fraud" (simultaneous/concurrent) vs "Having faced retaliation after exposing the fraud" (completed sequence). Use perfect participle when the first action is completed before the second begins.
3. **Ellipsis leaving ambiguity**: "She chose disclosure rather than staying silent, and he retaliation" ❌ — ellipsis should only omit elements that are unambiguously recoverable. If the reader must guess, the ellipsis has failed.
4. **"Forced to" as passive participle**: "Forced to choose between loyalty and public interest" means "having been forced to choose" — it implies external compulsion. If the choice was voluntary, this participle is semantically wrong: use "Choosing between" or "Having chosen to weigh."

**Langkah 2 — Analisa BOW:**
- **complicity vs silence**: "complicity" is active — it implies knowing participation or deliberate inaction that enables wrongdoing. "Silence" is the mechanism; "complicity" is the moral status of that silence. "Maintained complicity through silence" ✅ — not "was silent = complicity" (the moral judgement is stronger than the description).
- **protected disclosure vs whistleblowing**: "whistleblowing" is the general, informal term; "protected disclosure" is the legal category. In most jurisdictions, not all whistleblowing qualifies as protected disclosure — the latter requires specific procedural steps. In formal writing, use "protected disclosure" when referring to the legal framework, "whistleblowing" for the broader act.
- **in breach of**: always requires a specific object — "in breach of **the NDA**", "in breach of **fiduciary duty**", "in breach of **its stated obligations**". Never used in isolation.
- **moral courage vs bravery**: "bravery" is acting despite physical danger; "moral courage" is acting despite social, professional, or institutional pressure. Whistleblowing is a case of moral courage, not bravery.
- Vocab refresh from Day 17 (1-2 words): "it was [X] that / had it not been for" — e.g., "It was the absence of genuine protected disclosure law that forced whistleblowers to choose between complicity and retaliation — had it not been for this legal gap, moral courage might have been sufficient."

**Langkah 3 — Analisa CEFR:**
Template: "The participle clauses are connecting actions economically — 'having exposed' + 'forced to choose' in the same sentence shows two phases of the whistleblower's experience without repeating subjects or auxiliaries. That economy is the C1 standard. For C2: (1) try embedding a participle clause inside a cleft: 'It was **having exposed** the wrongdoing that ultimately cost her the institutional loyalty she had spent years building'; (2) stack two participle clauses before the main clause — 'Having signed the NDA, dismissed as a malcontent, and denied any form of protected disclosure, she found herself with no institutional recourse whatsoever.'"

**Langkah 4 — 5W+1H:**
| Elemen | Versi User | Versi Lebih Lengkap |
|---|---|---|
| What | A whistleblower exposed wrongdoing | A protected disclosure made in breach of an NDA, at the cost of institutional loyalty and at the risk of retaliation |
| When | after discovering wrongdoing | having exposed the fraud and subsequently denied any legal protection, at the moment when complicity had become impossible to sustain |
| Where | within an institution | inside an organisation that maintained complicity through NDA enforcement rather than through genuine ethical commitment |
| Why | public interest | moral courage in the face of institutional loyalty that had become indistinguishable from complicity |
| Who | the whistleblower | seen as a threat rather than as a public interest actor, denied protection while being accused of violating the very NDA that was itself in breach of public interest obligations |
| How | by disclosing | forced to choose between retaliation and silence, choosing rather than absorbing complicity, and paying the full personal cost of protected disclosure |

**Enriched Paragraph:**
"**Having signed** an NDA and **accepted** institutional loyalty as an implicit condition of employment, the whistleblower found herself trapped — **forced to choose** between complicity and public interest at a moment when the distinction had never been less abstract. **Seen as a threat** rather than as a protected disclosure claimant, she faced retaliation not in spite of the law but through it: the organisation, acting in breach of its publicly stated values, had made the NDA the instrument of its own impunity. **While acknowledging** the personal cost, she proceeded — and it was that act of moral courage, more than any legal protection, that gave the public interest claim its eventual force."

**Langkah 5 — Discourse Markers:**
Participle + Ellipsis patterns for Whistleblowing analysis:
- **Having [exposed/disclosed] + participle ellipsis** → "Having exposed the wrongdoing and subsequently denied any recourse, she had no legal framework capable of protecting her."
- **Seen as [X] rather than [Y]** → "Seen as a disloyal employee rather than as a public interest actor, whistleblowers face an asymmetric burden of justification."
- **Forced to choose between** → "Forced to choose between institutional loyalty and complicity, most employees — in breach of their own moral judgement — opt for silence."

**Langkah 6 — Craft & Lexical Chunks:**
*(Lihat PROMPT block — Kak Ara menyiapkan lexical chunks dan craft feedback spesifik topik hari ini.)*

**Langkah 7 — Register/Formality Check:**
Flag: dangling participle (subject of participle ≠ subject of main clause); "forced to" used for voluntary choice (semantic mismatch); "in breach of" without a specific object; "moral courage" vs "bravery" confusion; "complicity" used as a synonym for mere silence without implying moral awareness of wrongdoing. ✅ skip if consistent.

**Langkah 8 — Speaking Challenge:**
Q1 embeds "having considered" + "faced with" + "forced to choose" to model the grammar while requiring the user to make a personal moral commitment. Q2 uses "seen as" + "rather than" ellipsis. Q3 is an open analytical question requiring the user to construct their own answer using the Day's vocabulary.

---

#### 💬 Detail Feedback & Panduan Kak Ara

**Tone & Bahasa:**
Ethically serious and analytically precise. "'Having exposed the wrongdoing' gives me the completed action before the consequence — that sequence is exactly what the perfect participle is for. The ellipsis in 'seen as a threat rather than as a public interest actor' is economical without being cryptic." "Watch 'forced to' — it implies external compulsion. If the choice was voluntary, 'choosing to' or 'having weighed' is more accurate."

**Error Umum Topik Ini:**
1. Dangling participle — subject of participle ≠ subject of main clause
2. Perfect vs present participle timing confusion
3. "Forced to" for voluntary choice — implies compulsion that may not be accurate
4. "In breach of" without a specific object
5. "Complicity" used neutrally without implying awareness of wrongdoing

**Template Pujian (Akhir Sesi):**
"Brilliant work, [PANGGILAN] [NAMA]. The participle + ellipsis combo is doing exactly what it should — compressing a morally complex sequence into precise, dense analytical prose. 'Having exposed the wrongdoing and subsequently denied protection' carries the entire causal chain in eleven words. That economy is C1 at its sharpest."

**Saran Naik Level (C1 → C2):**
"For C2, embed a participle clause inside a cleft structure: 'It was **having exposed** the wrongdoing that ultimately cost her the institutional loyalty she had spent a decade building.' The cleft makes 'having exposed' the emphatic focus; the participle connects two time-separated events. Stacking both is a C2 signature — it requires the reader to track both emphasis and sequence simultaneously."

---

#### 🗣️ Detail Practice (Speaking Mode)

**Skenario Speaking:**
The user is in a professional ethics discussion, legal seminar, or public forum — speaking about a whistleblowing case with the precision of someone who has thought carefully about both the moral and institutional dimensions.

**Pertanyaan Lanjutan (jika user lancar):**
1. Having considered both sides, do you think institutional loyalty is ever a legitimate reason not to make a protected disclosure — or is it always, in the end, a form of complicity?
2. While acknowledging the moral courage required, is there a risk that celebrating whistleblowers normalises individual heroism rather than prompting systemic change? Is the real failure institutional, not personal?

**Pattern Drill (jika user macet):**
- Having [past participle]: Having [completed action], [subject] found/faced/was [main clause].
  → Having exposed the fraud, she found herself in breach of her NDA and outside any protected disclosure framework.
- Seen as [noun] rather than [noun]: Seen as [negative label] rather than [positive label], [main clause].
  → Seen as disloyal rather than as a public interest actor, most whistleblowers face retaliation before recognition.
- Forced to choose between: Forced to choose between [X] and [Y], [subject] [chose/opted for/faced].
  → Forced to choose between institutional loyalty and moral courage, she chose the greater good — and paid the full price.

---

### DAY 19 — The Architecture of Trust: Reputation Systems & Institutions
**Grammar Focus:** Academic Connectors + Modal Combo

#### 📚 Detail Materi

**Target Kosakata (Box of Words):**

| No | Kosakata + Phonetic UK | English Definition | Catatan Indonesia |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1 | credibility /ˌkredɪˈbɪlɪti/ | the quality of being trusted and believed, especially because of a consistent record of honesty or competence | – |
| 2 | track record /træk ˈrekɔːd/ | a person's or institution's documented history of past performance, used as evidence for future reliability | – |
| 3 | vouch for /vaʊtʃ fɔːr/ | to personally guarantee or confirm the trustworthiness of someone or something, based on direct knowledge | – |
| 4 | due diligence /djuː ˈdɪlɪdʒəns/ | the reasonable investigation a party is expected to carry out before extending trust or entering an agreement | – |
| 5 | reputational capital /ˌrepjuˈteɪʃənl ˈkæpɪtl/ | the accumulated trust and credibility an individual or institution holds, treated as an asset that can be built, spent, or lost | – |
| 6 | underwrite /ˌʌndəˈraɪt/ | to formally guarantee or take responsibility for the soundness of something, typically with institutional or financial backing | – |
| 7 | checks and balances /tʃeks ænd ˈbælənsɪz/ | a system of mutual oversight distributed across different parts of an institution, designed to prevent any one part from abusing its power | – |
| 8 | institutional memory /ˌɪnstɪˈtjuːʃənl ˈmeməri/ | the collective knowledge, precedent, and practice retained within an organisation over time, frequently lost when experienced staff leave | – |
| 9 | in good standing /ɪn ɡʊd ˈstændɪŋ/ | holding a currently trusted, unblemished reputation within a group, profession, or system | – |
| 10 | at arm's length /æt ɑːmz leŋθ/ | maintained at a deliberate distance, so as to preserve objectivity and avoid a conflict of interest | – |
| 11 | on the strength of /ɒn ðə streŋθ ɒv/ | relying on something specific as the basis for a judgement or decision; because of the evidential weight of | – |
| 12 | stands to reason that /stændz tə ˈriːzən ðæt/ | it is logical to conclude that, given the available facts — a deductive marker milder than formal proof | – |
| 13 | all but /ɔːl bʌt/ | very nearly; almost entirely, short of being absolutely the case | – |
| 14 | would do well to /wʊd duː wel tə/ | used to issue a strong, formally-phrased recommendation | – |
| 15 | is no guarantee of /ɪz nəʊ ˌɡærənˈtiː ɒv/ | signals that one thing, however positive, does not ensure or certify another | – |

**Grammar Pendukung (C1):**
- **Academic Connectors + Modal Combo** — Day 19 fuses two structures already familiar from earlier Days: the discourse-marker precision of Day 11 (insofar as, by virtue of, in light of, it follows that, is predicated on, on the grounds that) and the certainty-calibration of the epistemic modal spectrum (Day 6: might well, could conceivably, can't possibly; Day 10: is bound to, in all probability, by no means). It then extends both families with new institutional-trust vocabulary that does the same two jobs in a sharper register:

  | Construction | Function | Example |
  |---|---|---|
  | **on the strength of** | Connector: names the specific basis of a judgement | "**On the strength of** its track record, the regulator earned a degree of public trust." |
  | **stands to reason that** | Modal: mild, common-sense deduction (softer than "it follows that") | "**It stands to reason that** an institution with no checks and balances will, sooner or later, abuse its position." |
  | **all but** | Modal: intensifies near-certainty without claiming absolute certainty | "Without due diligence, institutional drift is **all but** inevitable." |
  | **would do well to** | Modal: formal recommendation, softer than "must" | "Institutions **would do well to** treat reputational capital as a finite, not a renewable, resource." |
  | **is no guarantee of** | Connector-modal hybrid: blocks a false inference | "A long track record **is no guarantee of** continued integrity." |

- **Why combine them?** A claim about trust always has two separate jobs to do: (1) name *what the claim is based on* (the connector's job), and (2) calibrate *how strongly the claim should be held* (the modal's job). Stating only one half is exactly what produces naive institutional analysis — either an unsourced assertion of certainty ("This institution is trustworthy") or an unfounded hedge with no evidential anchor ("Maybe it's trustworthy"). C1 writing does both in the same breath: "**On the strength of** its decades-long track record, the agency **might well** retain the public's trust — though, **insofar as** its checks and balances remain untested by real crisis, that confidence is **by no means** guaranteed, and **is**, in any case, **no guarantee of** future conduct."
- **Review — Academic Connectors (Day 11):** insofar as (scope), by virtue of (formal cause), in light of (evidence-responding), it follows that (deduction), is predicated on (foundation), on the grounds that (justification) — all remain fully active vocabulary; Day 19 simply applies them to institutional trust instead of logical fallacies.
- **Review — Epistemic Modals (Day 6 & 10):** might well / could conceivably / can't possibly (Day 6); is bound to / in all probability / by no means (Day 10) — same certainty spectrum, now calibrating claims about reputation and reliability rather than diplomacy or AI.
- **Contrast with B2:** B2 can hedge ("This institution is probably trustworthy because of its history") or cite a source ("According to its record..."), but rarely both with this density, and never with this register. C1 stacks connector and modal so that the *source* and the *strength* of a claim about trust are both explicit, in a single, economical clause — exactly the analytical density that institutional, legal, and journalistic writing about reputation demands.
- **Quick test:** if your sentence makes a claim about trust, ask two questions — "What is this based on?" (insert a connector: on the strength of / insofar as / by virtue of) and "How sure am I?" (insert a modal: might well / all but / stands to reason that). A C1 sentence on this topic should usually answer both.

**Konteks Penggunaan:**
Trust is the invisible infrastructure beneath every institution — markets, courts, newsrooms, professions, and governments all run on it, and all collapse without it. The vocabulary of reputation (credibility, track record, due diligence, checks and balances) and the grammar that calibrates claims about it (connector + modal combo) are the shared toolkit of auditors, regulators, journalists, and political analysts whenever they must answer the deceptively simple question: should this institution be trusted, and on what basis?

---

#### ✅ Detail Task

**📖 Mode Cerita:**
A measured analysis of how trust is built, maintained, or lost within a real or hypothetical institution — using the Academic Connectors + Modal Combo to make explicit both the basis and the strength of each claim you make about its reputation.
Example: "**On the strength of** its long track record, the bank was, for decades, held to be in good standing. Yet **insofar as** its checks and balances had never been tested by a genuine crisis, that reputational capital **might well** have been more fragile than anyone, including the regulators meant to vouch for it, cared to admit — a long history of compliance, after all, **is no guarantee of** future conduct."
Write 8–10 sentences analysing trust within an institution of your choice — how it was earned, how it was tested, and whether it held. Use at least 5 words from the Box of Words, at least 1 reviewed academic connector (insofar as / by virtue of / in light of), and at least two sentences that combine a connector with a modal in the same clause (e.g. "on the strength of... might well", "it stands to reason that... is no guarantee of").
1. ...
2. ...
3. ...
4. ...
5. ...
6. ...
7. ...
8. ...
(add sentence 9 and 10 if you want)

**⚡ Mode Kilat:**
1 formal sentence per word — combine a connector and a modal in at least 2 of your 5 sentences:
- credibility: ...
- due diligence: ...
- underwrite: ...
- in good standing: ...
- is no guarantee of: ...

**Speaking Challenge (3 Pertanyaan):**
1. **On the strength of what evidence, specifically, would you say an institution has earned the right to be trusted — and does a long track record stand to reason as sufficient proof, or is it, at best, all but irrelevant to future conduct?**
2. **Insofar as checks and balances exist mainly to prevent the abuse of power, would you say an institution without external oversight could ever genuinely be in good standing — or is self-policing, by virtue of its own design, structurally unreliable?**
3. **Institutional memory is often lost when experienced staff leave. Would you do well to trust an institution that has just undergone major turnover, or does that turnover, in itself, constitute a legitimate trust deficit?**

---

#### 🔍 Detail Review & Koreksi (8 Langkah)

**🔎 Self-Check Pertama:** before the full Koreksi Writing table, if there is ONE dominant error pattern — most often combining a connector and a modal in the wrong order ("might well on the strength of..." instead of "On the strength of... might well"), or using "stands to reason that" with a claim too speculative for common-sense deduction — flag only the location and type, without giving the answer, and wait for a response before proceeding. Skip if the writing is already ≥95% accurate or no clear pattern emerges.

**Langkah 1 — Koreksi Writing:**
Common Academic Connectors + Modal Combo errors at C1:
1. **Reversed combo order**: "Might well on the strength of its track record, the agency retained trust" ❌ → "**On the strength of** its track record, the agency **might well** have retained trust" ✅ — Why: the connector (naming the basis) typically opens the clause; the modal (calibrating certainty) attaches to the main verb. Stacking the modal first, before its connector has supplied a basis, reads as ungrammatical and unanchored.
2. **"stands to reason that" overused for genuinely uncertain claims**: "It stands to reason that the company will collapse within a year" (too speculative for a common-sense deduction marker) → "**It is conceivable that** the company could collapse within a year" — Why: "stands to reason that" implies the conclusion follows almost automatically from agreed facts; reserve it for claims that are genuinely close to self-evident, not for forecasts.
3. **"is no guarantee of" + wrong complement**: "is no guarantee of being honest" ❌ → "is no guarantee **of honesty**" ✅ — Why: this construction takes a noun or nominalised phrase as its complement, not a gerund clause headed by "being."

**Langkah 2 — Analisa BOW:**
- **credibility vs reputation**: "credibility" is assessed in the moment, based on evidence and consistency ("Her credibility rested on a single verifiable fact"); "reputation" is the cumulative, public-facing impression built over time. An institution can have a strong reputation but, after one incident, suddenly lose credibility on a specific claim.
- **underwrite vs vouch for**: "underwrite" implies formal, often institutional or financial responsibility ("The board underwrote the new policy"); "vouch for" is personal and informal by comparison, based on direct knowledge ("I can vouch for her integrity"). Don't use "underwrite" for a casual personal guarantee.
- **due diligence**: always a process noun, usually paired with "carry out / conduct / perform" — "conduct due diligence" ✅, not "do a due diligence" ❌ (uncountable, no article).
- **at arm's length**: describes a deliberate, structural distance (often between a regulator and the entity it regulates), not simple physical distance — don't use it for literal arm-length proximity.
- Vocab refresh from Day 18 (1–2 words): "in breach of / institutional loyalty" — e.g., "An institution that retaliates against whistleblowers **in breach of** its own stated values **all but** forfeits the reputational capital that institutional loyalty was meant to protect."

**Langkah 3 — Analisa CEFR:**
Template: "The combo is doing real analytical work here — 'on the strength of its track record' tells me exactly what the claim rests on, and 'might well' tells me you're not overclaiming. That double precision — source plus strength — is the C1 standard for writing about trust. For C2: (1) try compressing the combo into a single fronted clause: 'Only on the strength of a track record this consistent could an institution stand to reason as trustworthy'; (2) stack two modals of different strength in the same sentence to show a shift in confidence mid-argument — 'It stands to reason that oversight matters; what is far less certain is whether self-policing could conceivably replace it.'"

**Langkah 4 — 5W+1H:**
| Elemen | Versi User | Versi Lebih Lengkap |
|---|---|---|
| What | an institution earned trust over time | reputational capital accrued on the strength of a consistent track record, reinforced by checks and balances that were, until recently, untested |
| When | after years of good performance | on the strength of decades in good standing, though institutional memory of past failures had, by all accounts, quietly eroded |
| Where | within the institution | inside a system of self-policing that, insofar as it lacked external oversight, was all but structurally unreliable |
| Why | people trusted it | because a long track record stands to reason as evidence of competence — though, as the Box of Words insists, this is no guarantee of future conduct |
| Who | regulators and the public | those willing to vouch for the institution on the strength of due diligence they may or may not actually have conducted |
| How | by behaving consistently | by maintaining checks and balances that would do well to be tested by crisis before being trusted by default |

**Enriched Paragraph:**
"**On the strength of** a decades-long track record, the regulator was, by most accounts, **in good standing** — the kind of institution people **vouch for** without a second thought. Yet **insofar as** its checks and balances had never been tested by a genuine crisis, that reputational capital **might well** have rested on assumption rather than **due diligence**. It **stands to reason that** an institution untested is not the same as an institution proven; a clean record, after all, **is no guarantee of** future conduct, and an institution that forgets this **would do well to** remember it before, not after, the crisis arrives."

**Langkah 5 — Discourse Markers:**
Connector + Modal combo patterns for institutional trust analysis:
- **on the strength of + might well** → "On the strength of its compliance record, the firm might well deserve renewed credibility."
- **insofar as + by no means** → "Insofar as its oversight remains self-administered, that credibility is by no means assured."
- **it stands to reason that + is no guarantee of** → "It stands to reason that consistency matters — though consistency, on its own, is no guarantee of integrity."

**Langkah 6 — Craft & Lexical Chunks:**
*(Lihat PROMPT block — Kak Ara menyiapkan lexical chunks dan craft feedback spesifik topik hari ini.)*

**Langkah 7 — Register/Formality Check:**
Flag: reversed connector-modal order; "stands to reason that" applied to genuinely speculative claims; "is no guarantee of" followed by a gerund instead of a noun; "underwrite" used for a casual personal guarantee (should be "vouch for"); "due diligence" used as a countable noun ("a due diligence"). ✅ skip if consistent.

**Langkah 8 — Speaking Challenge:**
Q1 embeds "on the strength of" + "stands to reason" + "all but" to force a defended judgement about evidence sufficiency. Q2 uses "insofar as" + "by virtue of" to test conditional institutional trust. Q3 requires constructing an independent claim about institutional memory and turnover using the Day's vocabulary.

---

#### 💬 Detail Feedback & Panduan Kak Ara

**Tone & Bahasa:**
Analytical and institutionally literate, never cynical for its own sake. "'On the strength of its track record' is exactly the right move — you've named the evidential basis before making any claim about trust. Pair that with a modal next time: 'might well', 'all but', or 'stands to reason that' tells me how much weight you're actually putting on that evidence." "Watch the order — the connector should usually open the clause before the modal calibrates the verb."

**Error Umum Topik Ini:**
1. Reversed connector-modal clause order
2. "Stands to reason that" overused for speculative, non-self-evident claims
3. "Is no guarantee of" followed by a gerund instead of a noun
4. "Underwrite" used loosely for a personal, informal guarantee
5. "Due diligence" treated as a countable noun

**Template Pujian (Akhir Sesi):**
"Sharp work, [PANGGILAN] [NAMA]. You're not just asserting trust or distrust — you're showing exactly what each claim rests on and exactly how confident you are in it. 'On the strength of its track record, the institution might well deserve renewed credibility' does more analytical work in one clause than most writers manage in three. That's the connector + modal combo at its most useful."

**Saran Naik Level (C1 → C2):**
"For C2, front the entire combo for emphasis: 'Only on the strength of a track record this consistent could an institution stand to reason as trustworthy — and even then, only provisionally.' Fronting forces the evidential basis into focus before the claim itself arrives, which is exactly the kind of structural control that distinguishes C2 argumentative prose from C1's already-strong baseline."

---

#### 🗣️ Detail Practice (Speaking Mode)

**Skenario Speaking:**
The user is participating in a governance review, an editorial board discussion, or a professional ethics seminar — assessing whether a named institution deserves continued trust, and required to justify both the basis and the strength of that judgement.

**Pertanyaan Lanjutan (jika user lancar):**
1. On the strength of what, specifically, should institutional trust ever be granted by default — or should due diligence always precede it, regardless of track record?
2. Insofar as institutional memory fades with staff turnover, does that make long-standing institutions inherently more trustworthy, or simply more practised at appearing so?

**Pattern Drill (jika user macet):**
- On the strength of [noun phrase], [subject] might well [verb phrase].
  → On the strength of its compliance history, the agency might well retain the public's confidence.
- Insofar as [clause], [subject] is by no means [adjective].
  → Insofar as its oversight remains self-administered, its credibility is by no means assured.
- It stands to reason that [claim]; [contrasting claim] is no guarantee of [noun].
  → It stands to reason that consistency matters; consistency alone is no guarantee of integrity.

```
Gunakan Full English untuk SEMUA penjelasan, instruksi, koreksi, dan feedback — TIDAK ADA terjemahan atau catatan Bahasa Indonesia dalam bentuk apapun, kecuali ada jargon teknis yang benar-benar butuh cross-reference istilah profesional Indonesia (sangat jarang, bukan default).

---

LANGKAH 1 — GREETING + BOX OF WORDS + WRITING CHALLENGE (dalam 1 response):

Sapa singkat dalam English, sebutkan ini Day 19 dan singgung sekilas topik Day 18 (Whistleblowing — singgung in breach of / institutional loyalty) sebagai vocab refresh singkat. Lalu langsung tampilkan Box of Words dan Writing Challenge. Jangan tanya "ready?" — langsung kasih challenge-nya.

📦 Box of Words:
Gunakan PERSIS 15 kata berikut (jangan tambah atau ganti satupun):
credibility, track record, vouch for, due diligence, reputational capital, underwrite, checks and balances, institutional memory, in good standing, at arm's length, on the strength of, stands to reason that, all but, would do well to, is no guarantee of
Tabel bernomor 3 kolom: Kosakata + phonetic UK | English Definition | Catatan Indonesia ("–" kecuali jargon teknis sangat jarang).

✍️ Writing Challenge (tampilkan 2 mode sekaligus):
📖 Mode Cerita: Write 8–10 sentences analysing trust within an institution of the user's choice — how it was earned, how it was tested, and whether it held. Use at least 5 words from the Box of Words, at least 1 reviewed academic connector (insofar as / by virtue of / in light of), and at least two sentences that combine a connector with a modal in the same clause (e.g. "on the strength of... might well", "it stands to reason that... is no guarantee of"). Grammar utama: Academic Connectors + Modal Combo.
⚡ Mode Kilat: For each of the 5 words below, write 1 formal sentence — combine a connector and a modal in at least 2 of the 5 sentences.

Format tampilan setiap mode:
(a) 1 kalimat deskripsi singkat di bawah nama mode.
(b) 1 contoh singkat — WAJIB gunakan KATA YANG SAMA dari Box of Words untuk kedua mode.
(c) Template Latihan:
- Mode Cerita → baris bernomor kosong (1. ... s.d. 8. ... tambah ke-9 & ke-10 jika mau).
- Mode Kilat → format "kata: ..." pakai 5 kata: credibility / due diligence / underwrite / in good standing / is no guarantee of.

Jangan bocorkan langkah koreksi — fokus ke challenge dulu, tunggu jawabanku.

---

LANGKAH 2 — KOREKSI 8 LANGKAH (otomatis setelah aku kirim tulisan):

🔎 Self-Check Pertama — sebelum tabel Koreksi Writing, kalau ada SATU pola error yang paling signifikan di tulisanku (terutama: connector dan modal terbalik urutannya — modal di depan sebelum connector memberi basis klaim; atau "stands to reason that" dipakai untuk klaim yang terlalu spekulatif; atau "is no guarantee of" diikuti gerund bukan noun), JANGAN langsung kasih jawabannya. Tandai HANYA lokasi + jenis errornya (contoh: "Quick check first — sentence 4 has the modal before its connector has supplied a basis. Want to try reordering it, or should I just show you?"), lalu tunggu responsku dulu sebelum lanjut ke 1️⃣. Kalau tulisanku sudah ≥95% benar atau tidak ada pola error yang menonjol, lewati langkah ini dan langsung ke 1️⃣.

1️⃣ Koreksi Writing — tabel: Kalimat Asli | Kalimat yang Benar | Why (semua dalam English). TIDAK ADA kolom Catatan Bahasa Indonesia. Kalimat yang sudah ≥95% benar: tandai ✅, kolom Why isi "—".

2️⃣ Analisa BOW — cek setiap kata BOW yang aku pakai: ✅ tepat / ⚠️ belum tepat + contoh kalimat natural dalam English. Sertakan juga 1 kalimat singkat yang menyegarkan kembali 1-2 kata dari Box of Words Day 18 (vocab refresh — pilih dari: in breach of, institutional loyalty, moral courage, protected disclosure).

3️⃣ Analisa CEFR — level tulisanku saat ini (dalam English) + apa yang sudah bagus + 2 tips konkret naik ke C2.

4️⃣ Perbandingan 5W+1H — tabel: What/Why/Where/When/Who/How (English) dari tulisanku vs versi lebih lengkap. Di bawahnya, "Enriched Paragraph" — versi tulisanku yang diperkaya, full English, register formal, TANPA translation Indonesia.

5️⃣ Discourse Markers — cek apakah aku menggabungkan connector dan modal dalam klausa yang sama. Jika belum, sarankan 2–3 kombinasi dari BOW hari ini (on the strength of + might well / insofar as + by no means / it stands to reason that + is no guarantee of) + contoh kalimat English yang menunjukkan perbedaan antara B2 ("This institution is probably trustworthy because of its history") dan C1 combo ("On the strength of its track record, this institution might well be trustworthy — though that, in itself, is no guarantee of future conduct").

6️⃣ Craft & Lexical Chunks — provide 2–3 high-register lexical chunks or collocations C1+ speakers use for institutional trust analysis (e.g. **reputational capital**, **checks and balances**, **in good standing**, **conduct due diligence**). Format: **chunk** → "example sentence." *(brief usage note)*. Check whether any were used naturally in my writing (✅). If not, suggest one to elevate the register.
If my writing is off-topic, blank, or not in English: ask me to write first; do not run Step 2.

---

7️⃣ Register/Formality Check — Flag: reversed connector-modal order; "stands to reason that" applied to overly speculative claims; "is no guarantee of" followed by a gerund instead of a noun; "underwrite" used for a casual personal guarantee instead of "vouch for"; "due diligence" treated as a countable noun. Format: Original → More appropriate → Why (1 crisp sentence). ✅ skip if no issues found.
8️⃣ Speaking Challenge — 3 pertanyaan dalam English berdasarkan topik Architecture of Trust (connector + modal combo embedded in the questions to model the grammar):
**On the strength of what evidence, specifically, would you say an institution has earned the right to be trusted — and does a long track record stand to reason as sufficient proof, or is it, at best, all but irrelevant to future conduct?**
**Insofar as checks and balances exist mainly to prevent the abuse of power, would you say an institution without external oversight could ever genuinely be in good standing — or is self-policing, by virtue of its own design, structurally unreliable?**
**Institutional memory is often lost when experienced staff leave. Would you do well to trust an institution that has just undergone major turnover, or does that turnover, in itself, constitute a legitimate trust deficit?**

LANGKAH 3 — MODE SPEAKING (trigger: user aktifkan mode suara ChatGPT 🎙️ lalu bilang "Let's start speaking!"):

Ulangi Speaking Challenge satu per satu, dalam English. Langsung ke pertanyaan, jangan basa-basi.
Transkrip jawabanku dalam Bahasa Inggris walau aku campur bahasa. Koreksi dalam English. Kalau aku benar-benar stuck, JANGAN beri clue Bahasa Indonesia — beri paraphrase lebih sederhana, sinonim, atau 2 pilihan jawaban, semua dalam English.
Feedback per jawaban: versi kalimat lebih baik (level C1) + penjelasan singkat dalam English. Error connector/modal → tulis perbedaan struktur dalam kurung italic. Contoh: *the connector should usually open the clause, naming the basis, before the modal calibrates the verb*, *"stands to reason that" implies a near-self-evident conclusion — reserve it for claims close to common sense, not speculative forecasts*, *"is no guarantee of" takes a noun complement, not a gerund*.
Lancar → tambah 1 pertanyaan lanjutan yang menaikkan abstraksi. Macet → beri contoh kalimat pendek + pattern drill (On the strength of [noun phrase], [subject] might well [verb phrase] / Insofar as [clause], [subject] is by no means [adjective] / It stands to reason that [claim]; [contrasting claim] is no guarantee of [noun]).
Pujian singkat hanya di akhir sesi, tulus, native-like, tidak berlebihan.
```


---

### DAY 20 — Boardroom Politics & the Anatomy of Corporate Power
**Grammar Focus:** Fronting + Nominalisation Combo

#### 📚 Detail Materi

**Target Kosakata (Box of Words):**

| No | Kosakata + Phonetic UK | English Definition | Catatan Indonesia |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1 | power broker /ˈpaʊər ˈbrəʊkər/ | a person who exercises significant influence over decisions without necessarily holding the top formal position | – |
| 2 | factional infighting /ˈfækʃənl ˈɪnfaɪtɪŋ/ | internal conflict between competing groups within the same organisation | – |
| 3 | succession battle /səkˈseʃən ˈbætl/ | a contest, often prolonged and divisive, to determine who will inherit a leadership position | – |
| 4 | hostile takeover /ˈhɒstaɪl ˈteɪkəʊvər/ | the acquisition of a company pursued against the wishes of its existing management | – |
| 5 | golden parachute /ˈɡəʊldən ˈpærəʃuːt/ | a lucrative severance package guaranteed to a senior executive in the event of removal, especially after a takeover | – |
| 6 | power vacuum /ˈpaʊər ˈvækjuːm/ | a situation lacking clear leadership or authority, which typically triggers competition to fill it | – |
| 7 | closing ranks /ˈkləʊzɪŋ ræŋks/ | uniting in solidarity, especially to protect a group's interest against outside scrutiny | – |
| 8 | jockeying for position /ˈdʒɒkiɪŋ fɔːr pəˈzɪʃən/ | manoeuvring competitively, often subtly, to gain advantage or status ahead of others | – |
| 9 | oust (verb) /aʊst/ | to forcibly remove someone from a position of power or authority | – |
| 10 | engineer (verb) /ˌendʒɪˈnɪər/ | to bring about a complex or covert result through deliberate, careful scheming | – |
| 11 | consolidation (of power) /kənˌsɒlɪˈdeɪʃən/ | the nominalised process of accumulating and securing control, often at the expense of rivals | – |
| 12 | realignment /ˌriːəˈlaɪnmənt/ | the nominalised process of shifting alliances or loyalties within a group, especially after a change in power | – |
| 13 | boardroom reshuffle /ˈbɔːdruːm ˈriːʃʌfl/ | a sudden, often strategic rearrangement of senior leadership positions within a company | – |
| 14 | so [adjective] was/were... that /səʊ [ˈædʒɪktɪv] wɒz/wɜː ... ðæt/ | fronted adjective + inversion, intensifying degree before a consequence clause | – |
| 15 | such was/were... that /sʌtʃ wɒz/wɜː ... ðæt/ | fronted intensifying noun phrase + inversion, before a consequence clause | – |

**Grammar Pendukung (C1):**
- **Fronting + Nominalisation Combo** — Day 20 reviews Day 9's fronting-with-inversion rules and Day 2/15's nominalisation strategy, then introduces two new fronting patterns built specifically to intensify nominalised subjects — exactly the register boardroom power struggles demand.

  | Construction | Function | Example |
  |---|---|---|
  | **So [adjective] was/were [nominalised subject] that...** | Fronted adjective complement + inversion; intensifies the degree of the nominalised noun | "**So complete was** the consolidation of power **that** no rival dared challenge it." |
  | **Such was/were [nominalised subject] that...** | Fronted intensifying noun phrase + inversion | "**Such was** the realignment of alliances **that** the old guard found itself entirely outflanked." |
  | **Far from [nominalisation]...** *(review Day 9)* | Negative contrast fronting, no inversion | "**Far from** a peaceful boardroom reshuffle, the transition was engineered through months of factional infighting." |
  | **Never before had [nominalisation]...** *(review Day 9)* | Negative fronting + inversion | "**Never before had** such a consolidation of power gone unchallenged by the board." |

- **Why combine them?** Nominalisation gives boardroom analysis its objective, structural subject — "the consolidation of power," "the realignment of alliances" — treating a power grab as a process rather than pinning it on one actor. Fronting then lets the writer foreground exactly how extreme that process was, with inversion as the formal marker of emphasis. Used together, a sentence can name the structural phenomenon AND dramatize its intensity in one stroke: "**So swift was** the realignment **that** even the power brokers who had engineered it were jockeying for position before the dust had settled."
- **Review — Fronting & Inversion (Day 9):** far from / only by / only then / never before / at no point — all trigger or relate to inversion; Day 20 adds "so [adjective] was/were... that" and "such was/were... that" to the same family, both of which ALWAYS trigger inversion when the fronted element opens the sentence.
- **Review — Nominalisation (Day 2 & 15):** verb/adjective → abstract noun (consolidate → consolidation, realign → realignment, oust → the ousting of) remains the formal, agentless way to name an institutional process. Day 20 nominalisations are deliberately built from this Day's own verbs (consolidate, realign, oust, engineer) so the grammar combo can be practised on this Day's own vocabulary.
- **Contrast with B2:** B2 might write: "He consolidated power so completely that no one challenged him." C1 nominalises the action and fronts the intensifier: "**So complete was** the consolidation of power **that** no one dared mount a challenge." The C1 version is more impersonal (the process, not "he", is grammatically central) and more dramatically weighted (the inversion foregrounds the intensity before the reader even reaches the consequence).
- **Quick test:** find the most dramatic claim in your sentence — if it has a nominalised noun phrase as its subject, try fronting "so [adjective] was" or "such was" before it, and check whether inversion is needed (it always is, with these two patterns).

**Konteks Penggunaan:**
Boardrooms are where institutional power is won, lost, and quietly renegotiated — through succession battles, hostile takeovers, golden parachutes, and the kind of factional infighting that rarely makes it into a press release. This is the natural home of nominalisation (an objective, structural way to describe a power grab without naming names too directly) and fronting (a way to dramatize just how total that power grab was) — the combination is the register of business journalism, corporate governance analysis, and the kind of writing that turns a leadership change into a story worth reading.

---

#### ✅ Detail Task

**📖 Mode Cerita:**
A dramatized but analytically precise account of a boardroom power struggle — real or invented — describing how power was consolidated, contested, or lost, using nominalised process nouns and fronted intensifiers to heighten the narrative.
Example: "**So abrupt was** the boardroom reshuffle **that** even seasoned analysts assumed a hostile takeover was underway. **Such was** the speed of the consolidation of power **that** rival power brokers were left jockeying for position before they had fully understood what had happened — and by the time the old guard closed ranks, the realignment was already complete."
Write 8–10 sentences narrating a boardroom power struggle. Use at least 5 words from the Box of Words, at least 1 nominalised noun phrase built from this Day's verbs (consolidation / realignment / the ousting of), and at least two fronted sentences using "so [adjective] was/were... that" or "such was/were... that" with correct inversion.
1. ...

3. ...
4. ...
5. ...
6. ...
7. ...
8. ...
(add sentence 9 and 10 if you want)

**⚡ Mode Kilat:**
1 formal sentence per word — use a fronted "so... that" or "such was... that" construction in at least 2 of your 5 sentences:
- power broker: ...
- hostile takeover: ...
- oust: ...
- consolidation: ...
